import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self):
        super(Model, self).__init__()

    def forward(self, Q: torch.Tensor, K: torch.Tensor, V: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        out = torch.nn.functional.scaled_dot_product_attention(Q, K, V)
        return out

batch_size = 32
num_heads = 32
sequence_length = 512
embedding_dimension = 1024

def get_inputs():
    Q = torch.rand(batch_size, num_heads, sequence_length, embedding_dimension)
    K = torch.rand(batch_size, num_heads, sequence_length, embedding_dimension)
    V = torch.rand(batch_size, num_heads, sequence_length, embedding_dimension)
    return [Q, K, V]

def get_init_inputs():
    return []

Since d_start = threadIdx.x*8, the writes to q_smem are contiguous per thread. No bank conflict for writing (each thread writes its own 8 contiguous floats, which map to 8 consecutive banks). For reading q during dot product, since all threads need all q values, each thread reads q sequentially in a loop. Within a warp, all threads read q[d] at the same time, which is a broadcast. No bank conflict. Great. So q_smem is useful. Now, what about storing the 512 scores? We can store them in shared memory `scores_smem[512]`. Each thread computes 4 scores and writes them to `scores_smem[j_start + offset] = score`. Then __syncthreads(). Then all threads can read any score. Algorithm: 1. Load q into q_smem (128 threads, each loads 8 floats). 2. Compute scores for 4 keys: For each key id k_id = 0..3: j = threadIdx.x * 4 + k_id score = dot(q_smem, K[b,h,j,:]) // loop over d with float4 loads scores_smem[j] = score __syncthreads() 3. Softmax: - Find max over 512 scores. Use block reduction. Each thread finds local max among a subset (e.g., 4 scores it computed, or other). Then warp shuffle and shared memory to find global max. - Compute exp(score - max) for each score. Store back to scores_smem (or a separate probs_smem). Each thread can compute 4 exps. - Sum all probs. Use block reduction. - Normalize: divide each prob by sum. Let's do this carefully. Actually, we can do: - thread t computes local max over scores_smem[t*4 + 0..3] (or over all scores in chunks). Better: each thread processes a chunk of scores. With 128 threads and 512 scores, each thread handles 4 scores. But for reduction, we need to find max across all 512. We can use a simple tree reduction in shared memory. Or we can have each warp find max over its 32 scores, then reduce across 4 warps. But scores are 512. Let's allocate 512 floats. Each thread loads 4 scores into registers. Then warp shuffle to find warp max (over 32 threads, but each thread has 4 values). We can have each thread first find its local max (4 vals). Then warp reduce (32 threads) using shfl. Then write warp max to smem[4]. Then one warp reduces the 4 values. Similarly for sum. Let's write it step by step: ``` float local_max = -INFINITY; for (int i = 0; i < 4; ++i) { int idx = threadIdx.x * 4 + i; float val = scores_smem[idx]; local_max = fmaxf(local_max, val); } // warp reduce max for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) local_max = fmaxf(local_max, __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_max, offset)); // now lane 0 has warp max __shared__ float warp_max[4]; if (lane == 0) warp_max[warp_id] = local_max; __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x < 32) { float block_max = (threadIdx.x < 4) ? warp_max[threadIdx.x] : -INFINITY; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) block_max = fmaxf(block_max, __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, block_max, offset)); if (threadIdx.x == 0) smem_max = block_max; } __syncthreads(); float m = smem_max; ``` Then compute exp and sum: ``` float local_sum = 0; for (int i = 0; i < 4; ++i) { int idx = threadIdx.x * 4 + i; float val = expf(scores_smem[idx] - m); scores_smem[idx] = val; // store probabilities local_sum += val; } // warp reduce sum for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) local_sum += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_sum, offset); __shared__ float warp_sum[4]; if (lane == 0) warp_sum[warp_id] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x < 32) { float block_sum = (threadIdx.x < 4) ? warp_sum[threadIdx.x] : 0; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) block_sum += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, block_sum, offset); if (threadIdx.x == 0) smem_sum = block_sum; } __syncthreads(); float inv_sum = 1.0f / smem_sum; ``` Then normalize: ``` for (int i = 0; i < 4; ++i) { int idx = threadIdx.x * 4 + i; scores_smem[idx] *= inv_sum; } __syncthreads(); ``` 4. Compute weighted sum (output channels): Each thread t handles channels d_start = t*8 to t*8+7. Initialize acc[8] = {0}. For j = 0..511: float w = scores_smem[j]; float4 v0 = *reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(&V_ptr[j * D + d_start]); float4 v1 = *reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(&V_ptr[j * D + d_start + 4]); // unroll or loop acc[0] += w * v0.x; ... Write acc to O_ptr[d_start..d_start+7] using float4 stores. But wait: each thread reading V[j][d_start..d_start+7] for all j. With 128 threads, d_start values are 0,8,16,...1024. For a fixed j, threads 0..127 read V[j][0..7], V[j][8..15], ... V[j][1016..1023]. This is perfectly contiguous across the block. Memory coalescing: thread 0 reads bytes 0..31, thread 1 reads bytes 32..63, etc. A warp reads 1024 bytes (32 threads * 32 bytes). That's 8 128-byte transactions, contiguous. Perfect. Similarly for writing output O. Now, the score computation: each thread reads 4 rows of K. Thread t reads K[j_start..j_start+3][0..1023]. In global memory, row j is at base + j*D. So thread t reads K[j_start][0..1023] (4096 bytes), then K[j_start+1][0..1023], etc. Since each thread reads its own rows, there is no cross-thread coalescing for K. But each thread's reads are sequential. The memory controller will handle 128 sequential streams. Sequential reads are good for cache lines and memory throughput. In L2, the rows are cached, and multiple threads reading different rows may cause some conflicts but it's okay. However, we can try to improve K reads by having all threads read K in a coalesced way and storing in shared memory. But then each key is needed by exactly one thread, so no reuse. It might not help. But let's consider: if we read K via float4, each thread reads 256 float4s per key (1024/4). For 4 keys, 1024 float4s per thread. 128 threads * 1024 float4 = 131072 float4 = 524288 floats = 2MB. This is the same as reading K directly. The main question is whether the memory controller can sustain high bandwidth with 128 independent streams. I think yes, because the H100 memory controller handles many independent requests and the L2 cache is large. The rows are 4096 bytes; each warp's threads read different rows, but the L2 cache line is 128 bytes. So each L2 access fetches a 128-byte chunk. The memory controller can fetch many cache lines in parallel. The throughput should be close to peak. One concern: register pressure. Each thread needs: - q values: not stored in registers, read from shared memory. - K values: read and consumed in a loop. We can keep 8 floats in registers (2 float4 loads at a time). - V values: similar. - scores_smem is shared. - acc[8] floats. Total registers per thread: maybe ~40-50. That's fine. Another concern: integer arithmetic for indexing. Need to be careful to avoid overflow. Let's write the CUDA kernel. Mapping: - `b = blockIdx.y / H;` Actually B=32, H=32, so BH = 1024. `bh = blockIdx.y; b = bh / H; h = bh % H;` - `n = blockIdx.x;` query index. - `t = threadIdx.x;` thread index 0..127. - Q, K, V, O pointers are passed as flat arrays. Data layout: Q is [B, H, N, D]. Strides: Q[b,h,n,d] can be accessed as `offset = ((b * H + h) * N + n) * D + d`. Similarly K and V. Output O same layout. q_smem size: 1024 floats = 4KB. We can allocate `__shared__ float q_smem[1024];`. scores_smem size: 512 floats = 2KB. `__shared__ float scores_smem[512];`. Also need smem for max and sum reductions (a few floats). We should use `__shared__ float smem_red[128];` or similar for reduction. But we can use warp shuffles. Wait, in the reduction, we need warp_max[4] and warp_sum[4]. We can use `__shared__ float warp_buf[4];` but since there are only 4 warps, we can just use `__shared__ float warp_buf[8];` or allocate in the same smem. Shared memory total: - q_smem: 1024 * 4 = 4096 bytes. - scores_smem: 512 * 4 = 2048 bytes. - warp_buf: maybe 8 * 4 = 32 bytes. Total: ~6.2 KB. Very small. This allows many blocks per SM (occupancy limited by registers and threads, not smem). Now, writing the kernel: ``` __global__ void attention_kernel(const float* Q, const float* K, const float* V, float* O, int B, int H, int N, int D) { int bh = blockIdx.y; int b = bh / H; int h = bh % H; int n = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; const int D4 = D / 4; // D=1024, so D4=256 // base offsets int qkv_bh = ((b * H + h) * N + n) * D; // for Q and O int kv_bh = ((b * H + h) * N) * D; // for K and V (row 0) const float* q_ptr = Q + qkv_bh; const float* k_base = K + kv_bh; const float* v_base = V + kv_bh; float* o_ptr = O + qkv_bh; __shared__ float q_smem[1024]; __shared__ float scores[512]; __shared__ float smem_buf[2]; // for max and sum // Load q into shared memory // Each thread loads 8 floats (2 float4s) for (int i = tid; i < D4; i += blockDim.x) { float4 q4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(q_ptr)[i]; q_smem[i*4 + 0] = q4.x; q_smem[i*4 + 1] = q4.y; q_smem[i*4 + 2] = q4.z; q_smem[i*4 + 3] = q4.w; } __syncthreads(); // Compute scores for 4 keys int j_start = tid * 4; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < 4; ++k) { int j = j_start + k; const float* k_row = k_base + j * D; float score = 0.0f; // dot product q_smem with K[j] #pragma unroll 16 for (int d4 = 0; d4 < D4; ++d4) { float4 k4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(k_row)[d4]; int d = d4 * 4; score += q_smem[d] * k4.x; score += q_smem[d+1] * k4.y; score += q_smem[d+2] * k4.z; score += q_smem[d+3] * k4.w; } scores[j] = score / sqrtf((float)D); // scale factor } __syncthreads(); // Softmax // Each thread loads 4 scores float local_max = -INFINITY; for (int k = 0; k < 4; ++k) { local_max = fmaxf(local_max, scores[tid * 4 + k]); } // Warp reduce max for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { local_max = fmaxf(local_max, __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_max, offset)); } __shared__ float warp_max[4]; if (lane == 0) warp_max[warp_id] = local_max; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 4) { float block_max = warp_max[tid]; for (int offset = 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { block_max = fmaxf(block_max, __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, block_max, offset)); } if (tid == 0) smem_buf[0] = block_max; } __syncthreads(); float m = smem_buf[0]; // Compute exp and local sum float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int k = 0; k < 4; ++k) { int idx = tid * 4 + k; float val = expf(scores[idx] - m); scores[idx] = val; local_sum += val; } // Warp reduce sum for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { local_sum += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_sum, offset); } __shared__ float warp_sum[4]; if (lane == 0) warp_sum[warp_id] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); if (tid < 4) { float block_sum = warp_sum[tid]; for (int offset = 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { block_sum += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, block_sum, offset); } if (tid == 0) smem_buf[1] = block_sum; } __syncthreads(); float inv_sum = 1.0f / smem_buf[1]; // Normalize scores for (int k = 0; k < 4; ++k) { int idx = tid * 4 + k; scores[idx] *= inv_sum; } __syncthreads(); // Weighted sum to compute output channels int d_start = tid * 8; float acc0 = 0.0f, acc1 = 0.0f, acc2 = 0.0f, acc3 = 0.0f; float acc4 = 0.0f, acc5 = 0.0f, acc6 = 0.0f, acc7 = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float w = scores[j]; const float* v_row = v_base + j * D; // load 8 floats as 2 float4 float4 v4_0 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(v_row + d_start)[0]; float4 v4_1 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(v_row + d_start + 4)[0]; acc0 += w * v4_0.x; acc1 += w * v4_0.y; acc2 += w * v4_0.z; acc3 += w * v4_0.w; acc4 += w * v4_1.x; acc5 += w * v4_1.y; acc6 += w * v4_1.z; acc7 += w * v4_1.w; } // Write output float4 o4_0, o4_1; o4_0.x = acc0; o4_0.y = acc1; o4_0.z = acc2; o4_0.w = acc3; o4_1.x = acc4; o4_1.y = acc5; o4_1.z = acc6; o4_1.w = acc7; reinterpret_cast<float4*>(o_ptr + d_start)[0] = o4_0; reinterpret_cast<float4*>(o_ptr + d_start + 4)[1] = o4_1; // Wait, indexing // v_row + d_start + 4, then cast to float4* means address = (v_row + d_start + 4) / 4 = v_row/4 + d_start/4 + 1. Since d_start is multiple of 8, d_start/4 is integer. So index 1. // Let's do: // float4* o4_ptr = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(o_ptr + d_start); // o4_ptr[0] = o4_0; // o4_ptr[1] = o4_1; } ``` Wait, indexing: `o_ptr + d_start` is a float*. Casting to `float4*` means the pointer advances by d_start/4. Then `o4_ptr[0]` covers d_start..d_start+3. `o4_ptr[1]` covers d_start+4..d_start+7. That's correct. Similarly for V loads: `v_row + d_start` cast to float4* covers d_start..d_start+3, then `[1]` covers d_start+4..d_start+7. Good. But for q_smem loads: we used `for (int i = tid; i < D4; i += blockDim.x)`. Since D4=256, blockDim=128, each thread loads 2 float4s. Thread tid loads i=tid and i=tid+128. That means thread 0 loads q[0..3] and q[512..515]. Threads in warp 0 load i=0..31 (q[0..127]) and i=128..159 (q[512..639]). This is coalesced: addresses are base + 0..31*16 and base + 128*16..159*16. Wait, q_ptr is a float*. `reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(q_ptr)[i]` accesses bytes i*16. So thread 0 accesses 0, thread 1 accesses 16, ..., thread 31 accesses 496. That's contiguous 512 bytes. Coalesced. Thread 128 accesses bytes 2048, thread 129 accesses 2064, etc. Also coalesced. Good. But does q_smem need to be padded? When writing to q_smem, thread tid writes to q_smem[i*4]..q_smem[i*4+3]. For i=tid, writes to q_smem[tid*4 .. tid*4+3]. Threads in warp 0 write to 0..3, 4..7, ..., 124..127. These are all different banks (bank = addr%32 = 0..31). No conflict. For i=tid+128, writes to 512..515, 516..519, etc. Banks = 0..31 again. But the writes are in a second loop iteration. So within one iteration, no bank conflict. Good. Similarly for score writes: each thread writes to `scores[j_start + k] = score`. j_start = tid*4. So threads write to 0,4,8,...508. These are spaced by 4. Bank = addr%32 = 0,4,8,... So 8 distinct banks, each bank written by 4 threads. That's a 4-way bank conflict. We can avoid by storing scores in a transposed way or padded. But score writes are only 4 per thread. 4-way conflict is not terrible. However, when all threads write to scores, it might be a few cycles. We could use float4 stores for scores, but scores are computed per thread one by one. We can pack them into a float4 and store. But since j_start = tid*4, thread tid writes scores[tid*4..tid*4+3] which are contiguous. A float4 store is possible if we compute all 4 scores first. That's doable! We have 4 scores in registers. We can store them as a float4: `reinterpret_cast<float4*>(scores)[tid] = score4;`. This is a single instruction per thread, and accesses are contiguous across the warp: thread 0 writes bytes 0..15, thread 1 writes 16..31, etc. Banks 0..31, no conflict. Great! Let's do that. But note: scores are a shared memory array of 512 floats. `reinterpret_cast<float4*>(scores)[tid]` writes 16 bytes (4 floats) starting at address `scores + tid*4`. For a warp, writes are to 0..15, 16..31, ..., 496..511. That's contiguous and no bank conflict. Perfect. Similarly for reading scores in the softmax step: `float4 s4 = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(scores)[tid];` reads 4 scores. Then local_max = max(s4.x, s4.y, s4.z, s4.w). Good. For storing back probabilities: `reinterpret_cast<float4*>(scores)[tid] = make_float4(expf(s4.x - m), ...);`. Good. For the weighted sum, we need to read `scores[j]` for j=0..511. Each thread reads all 512 scores? Wait, in the loop `for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j)`, each thread reads `scores[j]`. But `N=512`. So each thread reads all 512 floats from shared memory. That's 512 * 4 bytes = 2KB per thread. Across 128 threads, it's 256KB of shared memory reads, but since they are broadcast? Actually, all threads read the same `scores[j]` at the same time (for a fixed j). That's a broadcast! No bank conflict. Perfect. And it's fast. So the loop `for j=0..511` does 512 iterations. In each iteration, load a float w = scores[j] (broadcast), then load 2 float4 from global V, multiply-add. 512 iterations per block. With 524,288 blocks, total iterations = 268M. Each iteration does 8 FMAs per thread (plus loads). That's okay. But wait: `scores[j]` broadcast: 128 threads reading the same address. For a warp, it's a broadcast, efficient. For 4 warps, it might be slightly serialized but still fast. Could we loop over j in chunks and have each thread read a float4 of weights? Not helpful because the weights are the same for all threads. Potential optimization: read weights into registers in chunks before the loop? 512 floats is 2KB. Each thread has 255 registers max. 512 floats is too many. But we can read a float4 of weights and broadcast? Actually, each thread only needs scalar w per j. So broadcast is fine. Now, the dot product for scores: Each thread computes 4 dot products of length 1024. It loops over D4=256. For each iteration, it reads a float4 from K. That's 4*256 = 1024 float4 reads per thread. 128 threads => 131072 float4 reads. Coalescing: each thread reads a contiguous row. Not coalesced across threads, but sequential within thread. This should still achieve decent memory throughput. To improve, we can read K via float4, and unroll the loop. But we might also load K rows into shared memory in a coalesced way. Let's think if it's worth it. If we load K into shared memory, we need to read K from global memory anyway. The benefit would be better coalescing during the global load. But the per-thread sequential read is already good for utilizing cache lines. The only issue is that the memory controller may not combine requests across threads. However, H100 has a very capable memory subsystem. Maybe direct global reads are fine. But there is a subtlety: the dot product reads q_smem[d] and K[j][d]. The q_smem read is a broadcast (if all threads read same d) or at least same address across the warp? Wait, in the loop: ``` for (int d4 = 0; d4 < D4; ++d4) { float4 k4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(k_row)[d4]; int d = d4 * 4; score += q_smem[d] * k4.x; ... } ``` In this loop, all threads in the warp are executing the same iteration d4. They all read q_smem[d] (same address), which is a broadcast. They read K[j][d] where j differs per thread (since each thread handles different keys). So K reads are not coalesced, but each thread is reading sequentially. Could we transpose the loop to have all threads in a warp read the same K row and different q values? No, because each thread needs a different key. Unless we compute scores for multiple queries in a block. But with one query per block, each key is unique to a thread. Given that the naive approach is simple, let's prototype it and see if it compiles and passes correctness. Then we can try to optimize further if needed. One more thing: the scaling factor. In `scaled_dot_product_attention`, the scores are divided by sqrt(D). However, the default `scale` parameter is `None`, which means `1/sqrt(D)`. So we must include `score / sqrtf(D)`. D=1024. We can precompute `scale = 1.0f / sqrtf(D)` or `rsqrtf(D)`. Since D is constant 1024, we can compute `rsqrtf(1024.0f)` once per thread or use a constant. Also, `scaled_dot_product_attention` might have a `scale` argument, but the reference implementation calls it without scale. So default is used. Another thing: numerical stability. We compute max and subtract. We store exp(score - max). If score - max is a large negative number, exp underflows to 0. That's fine. Now, writing the actual `ModelNew` class. We need to use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` to compile the CUDA code. Python code structure: ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __global__ void attention_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ Q, const float* __restrict__ K, const float* __restrict__ V, float* __restrict__ O, int B, int H, int N, int D) { ... } torch::Tensor attention_forward(torch::Tensor Q, torch::Tensor K, torch::Tensor V) { auto O = torch::empty_like(Q); int B = Q.size(0); int H = Q.size(1); int N = Q.size(2); int D = Q.size(3); dim3 grid(N, B*H); dim3 block(128); attention_forward_kernel<<<grid, block>>>( Q.data_ptr<float>(), K.data_ptr<float>(), V.data_ptr<float>(), O.data_ptr<float>(), B, H, N, D); return O; } ''' cpp_source = r''' torch::Tensor attention_forward(torch::Tensor Q, torch::Tensor K, torch::Tensor V); ''' module = load_inline( name='attention_kernel', cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['attention_forward'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3', '--use_fast_math'], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(torch.nn.Module): def __init__(self): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() def forward(self, Q, K, V): return module.attention_forward(Q, K, V) ``` Wait, `load_inline` signature: `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline(name, cpp_sources, cuda_sources, functions, ...)`. The `cpp_sources` should contain the declaration of the functions to be exported. Also, we need to include `<torch/extension.h>` in CUDA? Actually, torch/extension.h is for C++ side. For CUDA source, we can include <cuda_runtime.h> and maybe <torch/extension.h> if we use ATen types. In the CUDA source, we can write plain CUDA C++ functions that take raw pointers. The wrapper function `attention_forward` should be in the cpp_sources or cuda_sources? Since we need to call `torch::empty_like`, we need to include <torch/extension.h>. We can put the wrapper in the cpp_sources or in the cuda_sources. Usually, we put everything in `cuda_sources` and include <torch/extension.h>. That's fine. Let's check compile errors. We need to be careful with reinterpret_cast on float* to float4* for shared memory or pointers that may not be aligned? In the kernel, Q, K, V, O are `float*` from tensors. PyTorch's float tensors are aligned to at least 16 bytes? Usually yes for the first element, but `k_row + d_start` where d_start = tid*8, and tid can be 127, so d_start = 1016. The base of V is aligned, but V[0][1016] might not be 16-byte aligned if the row length is 1024 floats = 4096 bytes. 1016 * 4 = 4064. 4064 % 16 = 0? 4064 / 16 = 254. Yes, it's 16-byte aligned! Since 1024 is divisible by 4, all columns are aligned to 4*4=16 bytes. So float4 loads/stores are safe. But `k_row` is a pointer to a row start (`K + kv_bh + j*D`). Row start is 4096-byte aligned if the base is 4096-byte aligned? The base pointer is aligned to at least 256 bytes for PyTorch float tensors. Actually, PyTorch tensors are typically aligned to 16 bytes or more. For contiguous tensors of shape [B,H,N,D], the stride between rows is D=1024 floats = 4096 bytes. So if the start of the tensor is aligned to 4096 bytes, then each row start is aligned. But we cannot guarantee 4096-byte alignment. However, float4 requires 16-byte alignment. Since each row start is offset by j*4096 bytes from kv_bh, and 4096 is a multiple of 16, the alignment of row start is the same as kv_bh alignment. If kv_bh is at least 16-byte aligned (which is true for float tensors), then float4 access is safe. PyTorch ensures alignment for vectorized loads. So we are good. Similarly for q_ptr, o_ptr. One issue: In the score loop, we do `reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(k_row)[d4]`. That's a float4 load from global memory. This requires k_row to be 16-byte aligned. As argued, yes. In the reduction for max/sum across warps, we used `__shfl_xor_sync` for block-level reduction with only 4 warps. The second reduction uses a warp of 4 threads (tid < 4). We need to pass a mask that covers those threads. `0xFFFFFFFF` includes all 32 threads, but only tid < 4 are active. `__shfl_xor_sync` requires all threads in the mask to execute the intrinsic. Since we have it inside `if (tid < 4)`, threads with tid >= 4 will be divergent and not execute it. That's a problem! The intrinsic must be called by all threads in the mask. If the mask includes threads that don't call it, behavior is undefined. We should have all threads in the warp call it, but pass a default value for inactive threads. Better: Use the whole warp for the second reduction. All 32 threads in warp 0 participate. ``` if (warp_id == 0) { float block_max = (lane < 4) ? warp_max[lane] : -INFINITY; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { block_max = fmaxf(block_max, __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, block_max, offset)); } if (lane == 0) smem_buf[0] = block_max; } ``` This ensures all 32 threads in warp 0 execute the shuffle. The other warps (warp_id > 0) just wait. Since we have `__syncthreads()` before, all threads are at the same point. The other warps will skip this block. But then we need another `__syncthreads()` after. However, warp 0 is doing work while other warps are idle. That's fine. But wait: the `if (warp_id == 0)` causes divergence. Only warp 0 executes the shuffle. The other warps do nothing. After that, warp 0 writes to smem. Then we need `__syncthreads()` so all threads see the result. But `__syncthreads()` requires all threads to reach it. Since only warp 0 entered the `if`, the other warps are already past it? No, other warps are not inside the `if`, so they proceed to the `__syncthreads()` immediately. That's fine. They will wait at the barrier for warp 0 to finish. But we need to be careful: __syncthreads() inside divergent code is not allowed if some threads in the block do not reach it. However, in this case, only the `if (warp_id == 0)` block contains shuffles and no `__syncthreads()`. The `__syncthreads()` is after the if, so all threads reach it. Good. Similarly for sum reduction. Let's refine the reduction code to be correct: ```cpp // Warp-level reduction for max float local_max = ... for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { local_max = fmaxf(local_max, __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_max, offset)); } __shared__ float warp_max[4]; if (lane == 0) warp_max[warp_id] = local_max; __syncthreads(); // Block-level reduction for max if (warp_id == 0) { float val = (lane < 4) ? warp_max[lane] : -INFINITY; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val = fmaxf(val, __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset)); } if (lane == 0) smem_buf[0] = val; } __syncthreads(); float m = smem_buf[0]; ``` Similarly for sum: ```cpp float local_sum = ... for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { local_sum += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_sum, offset); } __shared__ float warp_sum[4]; if (lane == 0) warp_sum[warp_id] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { float val = (lane < 4) ? warp_sum[lane] : 0.0f; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } if (lane == 0) smem_buf[1] = val; } __syncthreads(); float inv_sum = 1.0f / smem_buf[1]; ``` This is correct. Now, dot product computation: `q_smem[d]` is read by all threads. Since it's shared memory, broadcast is fast. Potential performance improvement: unroll the dot product loop. D4=256. Unrolling 16 or 32 might help. But 256 is large, manual unroll might increase code size. We can use `#pragma unroll 16`. That should be fine. But V loads are already vectorized (float4). There's not much more to do. Wait: the weighted sum loop reads V[j][d_start..d_start+7] for j=0..511. This is a matrix-vector multiplication where the vector is the attention weights. Since each row of V is 1024 floats, and each block reads all rows, the memory footprint is 2MB of V. But each block reads the same V data (for a given head) across 512 queries. As noted, L2 should handle it. However, there is a potential issue: if the 512 blocks for a given head are not scheduled back-to-back, L2 cache misses will occur. But given the grid dimensions, blocks with same y but different x are consecutive. The block scheduler usually processes in linear order, so they should be close in time. On H100, with 132 SMs and 512 blocks per head, each SM gets about 4 blocks per head. Actually total blocks = 524,288. If they are linearized as x + N*y, then all query blocks for head 0 come first, then head 1, etc. Or if linearized as y + BH*x? Actually CUDA linearizes 2D grid as `blockIdx.x + gridDim.x * blockIdx.y`. With gridDim.x = N = 512, gridDim.y = B*H = 1024. Linear order: - block 0: (x=0, y=0) -> query 0, head 0 - block 1: (x=1, y=0) -> query 1, head 0 ... - block 511: (x=511, y=0) -> query 511, head 0 - block 512: (x=0, y=1) -> query 0, head 1 ... So yes, all 512 queries for head 0 are consecutive. Great! L2 will hold K and V for head 0 for the first 512 blocks. Then head 1, etc. This is perfect for cache reuse. So each K and V per head is read from global memory at most once (or a few times) into L2. Given that, the memory traffic for K and V is just reading them once per head (2MB each) from global memory to L2, then L2 feeds the SMs. Q is also read once per query (4KB each) = 2MB total per head. Output is written once (2MB per head). So total global traffic = 6MB per head * 1024 heads = 6GB. At 3.35TB/s = 1.8ms. Plus compute overhead. So if our kernel is memory bound, we should be close to 2ms. But wait: each block reads K and V from global memory or L2? In our kernel, K reads are direct global loads. With L2, they hit L2 after the first few blocks. Similarly V reads hit L2. The dot product reads K rows sequentially (4096 bytes each). The L2 cache line is 128 bytes. So each K row read touches 32 cache lines. L2 has enough bandwidth to supply all SMs. Potential issue: L2 bandwidth is high but not as high as HBM? L2 bandwidth is much higher. So L2 hits are fast. Thus, the kernel might be quite fast. One more thing: `scaled_dot_product_attention` with fp32 on H100 might not be fused if there is no native fp32 fused kernel. It might use the math fallback with explicit matmuls and softmax, which would be slower due to writing the attention matrix to HBM. In that case, our kernel will be significantly faster. Even if PyTorch uses a fused kernel (like cuDNN), our custom kernel might have similar performance. But we need to beat it. Let's see if we can further optimize. Optimization ideas: 1. We have one after q load, one after score write, one after max reduction, one after sum reduction, one after norm. That's 5. Could we fuse some? For example, after computing max, we can compute exp and local sum in the same loop. But we need global max first. The two reductions require syncs. 5. Wait, `--use_fast_math` might affect numerical precision. `expf` and `rsqrtf` with fast math may have slightly lower precision. The tolerance is fp32: atol=rtol=1e-4. Fast math should be fine, but `expf` in fast math can have larger errors. To be safe, maybe avoid `--use_fast_math` and just use `-O3`. But `-O3` might not vectorize as well. One more thing: The default `scaled_dot_product_attention` in PyTorch might disable causal mask etc. Our kernel computes standard attention with scaling. Now, let's think about numerical equivalence. Our kernel computes: - score = dot(q, k) / sqrt(D) - max reduction - exp(score - max) - sum reduction - weight = exp / sum - output = sum_j weight_j * v_j This is exactly the standard attention. The reference may compute the same, but ordering of reductions might differ slightly (e.g., max over tiles). Our max is over all scores computed exactly. So softmax should be accurate. The only source of error is floating point associativity in the dot product and sum. Since the reference might use matrix multiplication (cublas) which partitions the sum differently, there could be slight differences. But tolerance is 1e-4. The floating point error due to summation order is at most ~1024 * epsilon * magnitude ~ 1024 * 1e-7 * 512 ≈ 5e-5. So within tolerance. But in our kernel, each thread computes a dot product of 1024 elements using a loop of 256 float4 steps. The reference kernel (cublas) might use a tree reduction or different accumulation order. The difference could be a few 1e-5. Should be okay. One more concern: In the weighted sum, we have `acc += w * v`. With 512 terms, accumulation order is per thread sequential. The reference might do it differently. Still should be within 1e-4. Let's implement and compile. Wait, in our kernel, each block handles one query. But we have launch config `dim3 grid(N, B*H)`. In CUDA, `gridDim.x` corresponds to the first dimension, `gridDim.y` to the second. `blockIdx.x` ranges 0..N-1, `blockIdx.y` 0..B*H-1. This matches. Let's write the full code. One more thing: `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` may need the sources as lists. Also, we need to ensure C++14 or 17. Usually default is fine. Also, we should guard against non-cuda input? The inputs are cuda tensors. Let's draft the kernel code: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> #include <float.h> __global__ void attention_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ Q, const float* __restrict__ K, const float* __restrict__ V, float* __restrict__ O, int B, int H, int N, int D) { int bh = blockIdx.y; int b = bh / H; int h = bh % H; int n = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid & 31; int warp_id = tid >> 5; const int D4 = D >> 2; const float scale = rsqrtf((float)D); // Base offsets for query/output and key/value matrices int qo_offset = ((b * H + h) * N + n) * D; int kv_offset = ((b * H + h) * N) * D; const float* q_ptr = Q + qo_offset; const float* k_base = K + kv_offset; const float* v_base = V + kv_offset; float* o_ptr = O + qo_offset; __shared__ float q_smem[1024]; __shared__ float scores[512]; __shared__ float smem_buf[4]; // for max, sum, etc. __shared__ float warp_max_arr[4]; __shared__ float warp_sum_arr[4]; // Load q into shared memory #pragma unroll 2 for (int i = tid; i < D4; i += blockDim.x) { float4 q4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(q_ptr)[i]; q_smem[i*4 + 0] = q4.x; q_smem[i*4 + 1] = q4.y; q_smem[i*4 + 2] = q4.z; q_smem[i*4 + 3] = q4.w; } __syncthreads(); // Compute scores for 4 keys per thread int j_start = tid << 2; float4 score4; // Process each of the 4 keys #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < 4; ++k) { int j = j_start + k; const float* k_row = k_base + j * D; float s = 0.0f; #pragma unroll 16 for (int d4 = 0; d4 < D4; ++d4) { float4 k4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(k_row)[d4]; int d = d4 << 2; s = fmaf(q_smem[d], k4.x, s); s = fmaf(q_smem[d+1], k4.y, s); s = fmaf(q_smem[d+2], k4.z, s); s = fmaf(q_smem[d+3], k4.w, s); } s *= scale; // store into float4 components (&score4.x)[k] = s; } // Write scores to shared memory as float4 reinterpret_cast<float4*>(scores)[tid] = score4; __syncthreads(); // ---- Softmax ---- // Load scores back as float4 for reduction float4 s4 = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(scores)[tid]; float local_max = fmaxf(fmaxf(s4.x, s4.y), fmaxf(s4.z, s4.w)); // Warp reduce max #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { local_max = fmaxf(local_max, __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_max, offset)); } if (lane == 0) warp_max_arr[warp_id] = local_max; __syncthreads(); // Block reduce max using warp 0 if (warp_id == 0) { float val = (lane < 4) ? warp_max_arr[lane] : -INFINITY; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val = fmaxf(val, __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset)); } if (lane == 0) smem_buf[0] = val; } __syncthreads(); float m = smem_buf[0]; // Compute exp and local sum float4 p4; p4.x = expf(s4.x - m); p4.y = expf(s4.y - m); p4.z = expf(s4.z - m); p4.w = expf(s4.w - m); float local_sum = p4.x + p4.y + p4.z + p4.w; // Warp reduce sum #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { local_sum += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, local_sum, offset); } if (lane == 0) warp_sum_arr[warp_id] = local_sum; __syncthreads(); // Block reduce sum using warp 0 if (warp_id == 0) { float val = (lane < 4) ? warp_sum_arr[lane] : 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } if (lane == 0) smem_buf[1] = val; } __syncthreads(); float inv_sum = 1.0f / smem_buf[1]; // Normalize and store back p4.x *= inv_sum; p4.y *= inv_sum; p4.z *= inv_sum; p4.w *= inv_sum; reinterpret_cast<float4*>(scores)[tid] = p4; __syncthreads(); // ---- Weighted sum ---- int d_start = tid << 3; // tid * 8 float acc0 = 0.0f, acc1 = 0.0f, acc2 = 0.0f, acc3 = 0.0f; float acc4 = 0.0f, acc5 = 0.0f, acc6 = 0.0f, acc7 = 0.0f; #pragma unroll 4 for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float w = scores[j]; const float* v_row = v_base + j * D; float4 v4_0 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(v_row + d_start)[0]; float4 v4_1 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(v_row + d_start + 4)[0]; acc0 = fmaf(w, v4_0.x, acc0); acc1 = fmaf(w, v4_0.y, acc1); acc2 = fmaf(w, v4_0.z, acc2); acc3 = fmaf(w, v4_0.w, acc3); acc4 = fmaf(w, v4_1.x, acc4); acc5 = fmaf(w, v4_1.y, acc5); acc6 = fmaf(w, v4_1.z, acc6); acc7 = fmaf(w, v4_1.w, acc7); } // Write output float4 o4_0, o4_1; o4_0.x = acc0; o4_0.y = acc1; o4_0.z = acc2; o4_0.w = acc3; o4_1.x = acc4; o4_1.y = acc5; o4_1.z = acc6; o4_1.w = acc7; reinterpret_cast<float4*>(o_ptr + d_start)[0] = o4_0; reinterpret_cast<float4*>(o_ptr + d_start + 4)[0] = o4_1; } ``` Wait, in the V load, we have `v_row + d_start + 4` and cast to `float4*`. The pointer arithmetic: `v_row + d_start + 4` is a `float*`. Adding 4 moves by 4 floats. Casting to `float4*` and indexing `[0]` reads 4 floats starting at that address. Since `d_start` is multiple of 8, `d_start+4` is multiple of 4, so alignment is 16 bytes. Good. One issue: the `for` loop unrolling in the weighted sum: `#pragma unroll 4` with N=512 means 512/4 = 128 copies of the loop body? That's a lot (128 copies). It might increase instruction cache pressure and register usage. Better to unroll by a smaller factor, like 8, or not at all and let the compiler decide. Actually 512 iterations is fine without unrolling. Or we can unroll by 8: 64 copies. Let's just not unroll or use `#pragma unroll 8`. I'll remove the explicit unroll for the j loop, or keep it as `#pragma unroll 8` to hint. But the compiler might not fully unroll 512. It's fine to leave it unannotated. Actually, for performance, unrolling can help because the loop body is small and we want to interleave loads. But 512 iterations might cause register spilling if fully unrolled. I'll remove `#pragma unroll` on the j loop. Another thing: `scores` shared memory is 512 floats = 2KB. We need to ensure indexing for `scores[j]` is correct. `j` goes from 0 to N-1 = 511. Good. One potential issue: float4 loads from `scores` using `reinterpret_cast<float4*>(scores)[tid]`. This assumes `scores` is 16-byte aligned. Since it's the first element of shared memory, it's aligned to the start of the shared memory region, which is aligned to at least 16 bytes? Shared memory addresses are aligned to the start of the allocation. However, CUDA does not guarantee 16-byte alignment for static shared memory arrays? Actually, the compiler aligns shared memory arrays to the alignment of the element type. `float` is 4-byte aligned. `float4` requires 16-byte alignment. But we are reinterpreting a `float*` as `float4*`. The base address of `scores` might only be 4-byte aligned. Is it guaranteed to be 16-byte aligned? In practice, CUDA aligns shared memory to 16 bytes for the start of the kernel's static allocation. But I'm not 100% sure. To be safe, we can declare `scores` as `__shared__ float scores[512];` and then use `reinterpret_cast<float4*>(scores + tid*4)`. The address `scores + tid*4` is 16-byte aligned if `tid*4` is a multiple of 4, which it is. But the base `scores` must be at least 16-byte aligned. For static shared memory, the base is aligned to 16 bytes. According to CUDA docs, "The alignment requirement for a type is automatically satisfied when the variable is declared with that type." But since we declare as float, alignment is 4. However, `__shared__ float scores[512];` has alignment 4. Then `scores + 0` is 4-byte aligned but might not be 16-byte aligned? Actually, the compiler may align the entire shared memory block to a larger boundary. Let's not rely on this. We can declare `scores` as `__shared__ float4 scores4[128];` which is 16-byte aligned. Then we can index `scores4[tid]`. This stores 128 float4s covering 512 floats. Let's do that for safety. Similarly for q_smem, we used float4 loads into q_smem. We declared `q_smem[1024]`. float4 loads from global memory write to `q_smem[i*4]` with i=tid. The address `q_smem + tid*4` is 16-byte aligned if tid is multiple of 1? tid*4*4 = tid*16 bytes. So for any tid, tid*16 is 16-byte aligned. But the base `q_smem` must be at least 16-byte aligned. Same issue. Let's declare `q_smem` as `__shared__ float4 q_smem4[256];` and access via `q_smem4[i]`. Then to read individual floats, we can do `float qx = reinterpret_cast<float*>(q_smem4)[d];` or `float qx = q_smem4[d/4].x` etc. Or we can keep `__shared__ float q_smem[1024 + 4];` to ensure alignment? But no guarantee. Better to declare `__shared__ float4 q_smem_vec[256];` and access individual elements. For reading a single float `q_smem[d]`, we can compute `int q_i = d >> 2; int q_rem = d & 3; float qd; switch(q_rem) ...` That's annoying. Or we can just use `reinterpret_cast<float*>(q_smem_vec)[d]`. The base address of `q_smem_vec` is 16-byte aligned. Then `reinterpret_cast<float*>(q_smem_vec) + d` is 4-byte aligned. Float4 loads into it: `q_smem_vec[i] = q4;`. This is fine. So let's change: - `__shared__ float q_smem_vec[256];` // float4 - `__shared__ float4 scores4[128];` // 128 float4 = 512 floats - Then `float* q_smem = reinterpret_cast<float*>(q_smem_vec);` - `scores4[tid] = score4;` and `float4 s4 = scores4[tid];` etc. - For reading `scores[j]`, we need `float* scores_ptr = reinterpret_cast<float*>(scores4);` then `scores_ptr[j]`. Let's adjust. Also, for V loads: `v_row + d_start` is aligned to 16 bytes because `d_start = tid*8`. But `tid*8*4 = tid*32` bytes, which is 32-byte aligned, so definitely 16-byte aligned. Good. Let's rewrite with these arrays. ```cpp __shared__ float4 q_smem_vec[256]; __shared__ float4 scores4[128]; __shared__ float smem_buf[4]; __shared__ float warp_max_arr[4]; __shared__ float warp_sum_arr[4]; ``` Load q: ```cpp float* q_smem = reinterpret_cast<float*>(q_smem_vec); #pragma unroll 2 for (int i = tid; i < D4; i += blockDim.x) { float4 q4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(q_ptr)[i]; q_smem_vec[i] = q4; } __syncthreads(); ``` Now q_smem is 1024 floats starting at aligned address. Similarly, score writes and reads: ```cpp scores4[tid] = score4; __syncthreads(); float4 s4 = scores4[tid]; ... scores4[tid] = p4; __syncthreads(); float* scores_ptr = reinterpret_cast<float*>(scores4); for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { float w = scores_ptr[j]; ... } ``` This is safe. // D / 4, D is a multiple of 4 (1024)\n const float scale = rsqrtf((float)D);\n\n int qo_offset = ((b * H + h) * N + n) * D;\n int kv_offset = ((b * H + h) * N) * D;\n\n const float* q_ptr = Q + qo_offset;\n const float* k_base = K + kv_offset;\n const float* v_base = V + kv_offset;\n float* o_ptr = O + qo_offset;\n\n __shared__ float4 q_smem_vec[256]; The kernel compiled and passed correctness! Now let me evaluate if it's fast enough. The problem is a scaled dot-product attention with fairly large dimensions: - B=32, H=32, N=512, D=1024 My custom kernel uses a naive approach where each block handles one query (n), and within each block, 128 threads work on the problem. For N=512, there are 512 blocks per head, BH=32*32=1024, so 524,288 blocks total. That's a lot of parallelism. However, the compute is: - Score computation: N * D = 512 * 1024 = 524,288 FMAs per query = ~0.5M - Weighted sum: N * D = 512 * 1024 = 524,288 FMAs per query = ~0.5M - Total per query: ~1M FMAs - Total for all queries: 32*32*512 = 524,288 queries * 1M = ~0.5 MFMAs (wait, that's not right, total flops = B*H*N*(2*D + 2*D) = 32*32*512*(2*1024 + 2*1024) = 32*32*512*4096 ≈ 2.15 GFLOPS) Wait, total matrix multiplies: - Q*K^T: B*H*N*D*N = 32*32*512*1024*512 = 274,877,906,944 = ~274 GFLOPs - Softmax*(V): B*H*N*N*D = 32*32*512*512*1024 = 274,877,906,944 = ~274 GFLOPs Total = ~549 GFLOPs of compute. My kernel does these in registers with unrolled loops. On H100 with 1979 TFLOPS FP16/FP32 tensor cores, and with peak FP32 non-TC of 67 TFLOPS. The reference `torch.nn.functional.scaled_dot_product_attention` on H100 probably uses Flash Attention, which is heavily fused and tiled. My kernel might even be slower than the reference if I'm not careful, because I have a lot of global memory reads without much tiling (only query is cached in SMEM). In fact, I'm reading K and V directly from global memory inside unrolled loops. That's very heavy on memory bandwidth. Each thread reads K row and V row from global memory, but each warp works on 4 keys. 128 threads cover N=512. So each block reads 512 rows of K (full K matrix) and 512 rows of V (full V matrix). That's all global memory reads without caching. The whole K and V are read once per query position. In total, for 524,288 queries, K and V are read 524,288 times each? No, that's not right. Actually, each block handles one query, so the number of blocks is B*H*N = 524,288. Each block reads the entire K and V matrices for that head (N*D each). So total memory traffic from global memory for K and V = 2 * B*H*N*N*D * sizeof(float) = 2 * 32 * 32 * 512 * 512 * 1024 * 4 bytes ≈ 2,199,023,255,552 bytes ≈ 2.2 TB. At H100's 3.35 TB/s memory bandwidth, the theoretical minimum time is ~0.65 seconds just for memory bandwidth. That's way too much. Wait, actually... each block reads the entire N*D K and V for one (b,h,n). So total reads are B*H*N copies of the K and V matrices per head. 32*32*512 * 512*1024 * 4 = 2.2 TB as calculated. That's huge. For Flash Attention, memory traffic is much lower. For a causal or non-causal attention with N=512, Flash Attention would tile both Q/K and P/V, keeping tiles in shared memory and doing the computation in blocks. It reuses data much more efficiently. My kernel is definitely going to be memory bound and probably much slower than the reference. To beat the reference, I need a better approach. Maybe I shouldn't write a naive custom kernel. Since the reference is just `torch.nn.functional.scaled_dot_product_attention`, I need to either: 1. Write a kernel that's better tuned than the reference for these specific dimensions 2. Or, use some clever optimization For small-to-medium sequence lengths like 512, flash attention is very good. But maybe for D=1024, a batched GEMM approach is also very efficient. `scaled_dot_product_attention` in PyTorch already maps to efficient cuDNN or FlashAttention kernels. If the reference is already using the highly optimized SDPA, my custom kernel needs to be competitive. Let me think about what I can do differently. For N=512, D=1024: - Q*K^T is a matrix multiply of size (B*H*N, D) * (B*H*N, D)^T = (B*H*N, D) * (D, B*H*N)... actually batched. Each batch (b, h) of size (N, D) * (D, N) - result (N, N). - This is a large batch of matrix multiplies. cublas would do this very efficiently. - Then softmax over the last dimension. - Then multiply (N, N) * (N, D). So the computation is 2 big GEMMs + softmax. The reference `scaled_dot_product_attention` on H100 probably uses FlashAttention or something very efficient. Can I optimize further? Maybe I can't beat the reference with a simple custom kernel. However, let me think - maybe I can use some specific optimizations for the dimensions. N=512 is small enough that a naive attention kernel with all K, V in registers/SMEM could be extremely fast. Let me redesign the kernel to be much more efficient. **Better approach: TILE-based attention kernel** Since N=512, we can process in tiles. For example, we can have each block handle a TILE_N x something portion. But on H100, PyTorch's SDPA probably delegates to cuDNN's fused attention for these dimensions. Let me think about whether I can use a different strategy. Actually, for H100, flash attention v2 or flash attention v3 is probably used. These are extremely optimized. It might be very hard to beat them. Wait, but the problem asks me to write a custom CUDA kernel that runs faster than the PyTorch reference. Maybe the reference isn't using the fastest backend for these dimensions? PyTorch SDPA on H100 with these dimensions (N=512, D=1024) should use Flash Attention if enabled. But maybe it falls back to math or efficient attention. Actually, PyTorch's SDPA has multiple backends (flash_attention, mem_eff_attention, math). For D=1024, flash attention typically works well. Let me think about whether I can use specific optimizations. Alternative approach: Use a block size that processes a tile of queries and all K/V, accumulating in registers. Actually, for this problem, N=512 is relatively small. We could potentially load K and V into shared memory within each block. K has size 512*1024*4 = 2,097,152 bytes (2 MB). V is another 2 MB. That's way too large for shared memory (227 KB per block max). Another approach: since N=512 is small, we could do the entire attention per (b,h) in a single block, loading K and V from global memory but only once per block. Wait, 512 blocks per (b,h) where each reads all K,V is wasteful. What if each block handles a TILE_N of queries (e.g., 8 queries), and then K,V are read once per block and shared among the 8 queries? That reduces K,V reads by 8x. Even better, with TILE_N = 64, we'd have 8 blocks per (b,h), each reading all K,V once for 64 queries. That reduces total K,V reads by 64x. Let me calculate: B*H=1024, N=512. - With TILE_N = 1: 524,288 blocks, each reading K=2MB, V=2MB (effectively, but actually with cache it might not be that bad, but each block reads K,V once). Total: 1024 GB. - With TILE_N = 64: 8,192 blocks, each reading K,V once for 64 queries. Total: 16 GB. - With TILE_N = 512: 1,024 blocks, each reading K,V once. Total: 4 GB. Wait, but global memory reads should be cached in L2. Since all blocks for the same (b,h) read the same K,V, the L2 cache would cache them to some extent. But with 32*32=1024 streams of blocks each requiring K,V data, L2 cache (50 MB on H100) might not hold everything. K+V per head is 4 MB. So L2 can hold maybe 12 heads worth. But we have 1024 heads running. So there will be lots of L2 misses. A tile-based approach where each block handles many queries is definitely better. **Optimized design:** - Each block handles TILE_N queries (e.g., 8 or 16) - BlockDim.x = 128 or 256 - Load Q tile of size TILE_N x D into shared memory - Iterate over K, V tiles - But even simpler: each block handles one chunk of queries and does a simple loop over all N keys, accumulating attentions. Wait, with TILE_N queries per block and D=1024, the Q tile in SMEM would be TILE_N * 1024 * 4 bytes. With TILE_N=16, that's 64 KB, which fits. With TILE_N=32, that's 128 KB, still fits. With TILE_N=64, that's 256 KB, which exceeds shared memory per block limit (227 KB). So TILE_N around 32 to 48 would work well. Let's try TILE_N=32. SMEM for Q = 32 * 1024 * 4 = 131,072 bytes (128 KB). That's fine. Now, each block handles D channels cooperatively. We'd have 128 threads, each handling 8 channels (1024/128 = 8). A better approach: - Do warp shuffle reduction within each warp (4 warps) to get 4 partial sums. - One warp reduces the 4 partial sums. - Store to scores[i][j]. ```cuda // warp shuffle reduction #pragma unroll for (int offset = 32; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { partial += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, offset); } if (lane == 0) smem_workspace[warp_id] = partial; __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x < 4) { float val = smem_workspace[threadIdx.x]; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 2; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0x0F, val, offset); // or 0xFFFFFFFF within warp 0 } if (threadIdx.x == 0) scores[i][j] = val * scale; } __syncthreads(); ``` Wait, only warp 0 (threads 0-31) needs to reduce the 4 values. ```cuda if (warp_id == 0) { float val = (lane < 4) ? smem_workspace[lane] : 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 2; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } if (lane == 0) scores[i][j] = val * scale; } __syncthreads(); ``` This is better - only one __syncthreads per key-query pair. Or use some other optimization Actually, I think the TILE_N=32 kernel with 64 threads and online softmax (no score buffer) is a solid approach. Let me write it out properly. Wait, another idea: use `__half2` or other vector types? No, we're doing fp32. What about using more threads per block to parallelize more? I used 64 threads for 2 warps. Could I use 128 threads = 4 warps, each handling 8 queries? Then TILE_N=32. - 4 warps, each handles 8 queries. - Q_smem = 32 * 1024 * 4 = 128 KB. - No score buffer (online softmax). - For each key j: - All 128 threads load their 8 channels of K[j] (1024 channels / 128 threads = 8 channels each = 2 float4) - Each warp (32 threads) independently computes scores for its 8 queries. - Each warp does online softmax for its 8 queries and accumulates outputs. Total blocks = 16,384. K+V reads per block = 4 MB. Total K+V = 64 GB. With 4 warps, each warp has 32 threads, each thread handles 8 channels. Warp reduction over 32 threads: ```cuda #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { s += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, s, offset); } ``` This works perfectly. Per query in a warp: ```cuda float k_reg[8]; // 8 floats, loaded from K[j] float m = -INFINITY; // online max float l = 0.0f; // running sum float o_acc[8] = {0}; // 8 output accumulators (for 8 channels) for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { // Load K[j, warp_lane*8:(warp_lane+1)*8] // For simplicity, assume each thread loads its 8 contiguous channels const float* k_row = K + kv_offset + j * D + lane_in_warp * 8; float4 k0 = ((const float4*)k_row)[0]; float4 k1 = ((const float4*)(k_row + 4))[0]; k_reg[0] = k0.x; k_reg[1] = k0.y; k_reg[2] = k0.z; k_reg[3] = k0.w; k_reg[4] = k1.x; k_reg[5] = k1.y; k_reg[6] = k1.z; k_reg[7] = k1.w; // For each of the 8 queries handled by this warp for (int iq = 0; iq < 8; ++iq) { const float* q_row = q_smem[warp_id * 8 + iq]; // or shared memory pointer float s = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < 8; ++d) { s = fmaf(q_row[lane_in_warp * 8 + d], k_reg[d], s); } // warp reduce #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { s += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, s, offset); } if (lane_in_warp == 0) { float score = s * scale; // Online softmax update float m_prev = m; float l_prev = l; float m_new = fmaxf(m_prev, score); float l_new = l_prev * expf(m_prev - m_new) + expf(score - m_new); // Update output accumulators float factor = l_prev * expf(m_prev - m_new) / l_new; float w = expf(score - m_new) / l_new; // We need to apply factor to all 8 accumulators // And add w * V[j, :] to accumulators if (j == 0) { // special case: initialize } m = m_new; l = l_new; } __syncwarp(); // so all threads in warp see updated m, l } } ``` Wait, online softmax requires updating the output accumulators each iteration. For each query, we maintain: - m: current max - l: current normalizing sum - o[d]: accumulated output for each channel When a new score s comes in: - m_new = max(m, s) - l_new = l * exp(m - m_new) + exp(s - m_new) - o_new[d] = o[d] * (l * exp(m - m_new) / l_new) + V[j, d] * (exp(s - m_new) / l_new) The rescaling factor applies to all channels. If lane 0 computes and stores m, l, then other lanes need to read them for the accumulation. A `__syncwarp()` ensures lane 0's writes are visible. For the accumulation: ```cuda if (lane_in_warp == 0) { float m_prev = m; m = fmaxf(m, score); float scale_prev = expf(m_prev - m); l = l * scale_prev + expf(score - m); // store w = expf(score - m) / l // store scale_o = (l * scale_prev) / l ? wait, l is updated. } __syncwarp(); // All threads load m and l from lane 0, or from registers if broadcasted ``` Actually, `__syncwarp()` ensures convergence, but lane 0's local variables aren't automatically visible to other lanes. We need to use shared memory or `__shfl_sync` to broadcast. ```cuda if (lane == 0) { float m_prev = m; m = fmaxf(m_prev, score); float scale_prev = expf(m_prev - m); float exp_s = expf(score - m); l = l * scale_prev + exp_s; w = exp_s / l; // weight for V[j] o_scale = scale_prev * (l_prev / l); // rescaling for previous outputs // Actually, from Flash Attention paper: // l_new = l_old * exp(m_old - m_new) + exp(s - m_new) // o_new = o_old * (l_old * exp(m_old - m_new) / l_new) + V * (exp(s - m_new) / l_new) } // Need to share m, l, w, o_scale with all threads in warp ``` Let me write it carefully: ```cuda float m_iq, l_iq; float o_reg[8]; // initialize m_iq = -INFINITY; l_iq = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < 8; ++d) o_reg[d] = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { // compute score (warp reduce) float s = ...; // partial dot product // warp shuffle reduce - after this, lane 0 has full score #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { s += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, s, offset); } if (lane == 0) { float m_prev = m_iq; m_iq = fmaxf(m_prev, s); float scale_prev = expf(m_prev - m_iq); float exp_s = expf(s - m_iq); float l_prev = l_iq; l_iq = l_prev * scale_prev + exp_s; // compute weight and old output scale float weight = exp_s / l_iq; float o_scale = (l_prev * scale_prev) / l_iq; // Store in SMEM for broadcast smem_buf[0] = weight; smem_buf[1] = o_scale; } __syncwarp(); float weight = smem_buf[0]; // broadcast from lane 0 float o_scale = smem_buf[1]; // Load V[j] for this thread's 8 channels const float* v_row = V + kv_offset + j * D + lane * 8; float4 v0 = ((const float4*)v_row)[0]; float4 v1 = ((const float4*)(v_row + 4))[0]; o_reg[0] = o_reg[0] * o_scale + weight * v0.x; o_reg[1] = o_reg[1] * o_scale + weight * v0.y; // ... } ``` This is clean! Only one `__syncwarp()` per key j, and no `__syncthreads()` across warps because each warp handles its own queries entirely independently. The 4 warps in a block operate on disjoint sets of queries and never need to communicate. Wait, do they need to communicate for anything? No! Each warp: - Reads its 8 query vectors from global memory into SMEM (or directly from global memory) - Computes all scores and accumulates outputs independently - Writes final output For Q loading: We can have all threads in the block cooperatively load Q tile into SMEM. Since there are 128 threads and 32 queries * 1024 floats = 32,768 floats. Each thread loads 32,768/128 = 256 floats = 64 float4. That's a lot but happens once per block. After Q is loaded, each warp accesses Q from SMEM. Actually, we can avoid loading Q into SMEM and have each warp load its queries directly. But loading Q once into SMEM is better because then all 8 queries per warp are accessed multiple times (512 times for score + maybe for... actually, with online softmax, Q is only accessed during score computation, which is 512 times per query. So each query vector (1024 floats) is read 512 times from SMEM, which is fast. From global memory, it would be 512 reads, which is 2MB of memory traffic per query or 1GB per block. With SMEM, we save that global memory traffic. Wait, Q for this block is 32 queries * 1024 * 4 = 128 KB. Reading this once from global memory and then 512 times from SMEM is much better than 512 times from global memory. So Q_smem is definitely worth it. **Full kernel design:** ```cuda #define TILE_N 32 #define WARP_SIZE 32 #define NUM_WARPS 4 #define THREADS (WARP_SIZE * NUM_WARPS) // 128 #define D 1024 #define D_F4 (D / 4) // 256 #define N 512 __global__ void attention_kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.y / H; int h = blockIdx.y % H; int tile_q = blockIdx.x; // tile index for queries int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid % WARP_SIZE; int warp_id = tid / WARP_SIZE; int q_start = tile_q * TILE_N; int bh = b * H + h; int qo_base = bh * N * D; int kv_base = bh * N * D; // Load Q tile into shared memory __shared__ float q_smem[TILE_N][D]; // 128 KB // Each thread loads (TILE_N * D) / THREADS floats // = 32 * 1024 / 128 = 256 floats = 64 float4 per thread int q_load_count = TILE_N * D_F4 / THREADS; // 32 * 256 / 128 = 64 for (int i = 0; i < q_load_count; ++i) { int idx = tid + i * THREADS; int q_row = idx / D_F4; int q_f4 = idx % D_F4; if (q_row < TILE_N) { int global_q = q_start + q_row; if (global_q < N) { float4 val = ((const float4*)(Q + qo_base + global_q * D))[q_f4]; ((float4*)q_smem[q_row])[q_f4] = val; } } } __syncthreads(); // only needed once! // Each warp handles 8 queries int queries_per_warp = TILE_N / NUM_WARPS; // 8 int q_local_start = warp_id * queries_per_warp; // Online softmax accumulators for each query in this warp float m_arr[8]; float l_arr[8]; float o_arr[8][8]; // [query][channel] => 64 floats! #pragma unroll for (int iq = 0; iq < 8; ++iq) { m_arr[iq] = -INFINITY; l_arr[iq] = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < 8; ++d) { o_arr[iq][d] = 0.0f; } } // Iterate over all keys for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { // Load K[j, :] into registers for this thread's channels // Each thread gets 8 channels = 2 float4 const float* k_ptr = K + kv_base + j * D + lane * 8; float4 k0 = ((const float4*)k_ptr)[0]; float4 k1 = ((const float4*)(k_ptr + 4))[0]; float k_reg[8] = {k0.x, k0.y, k0.z, k0.w, k1.x, k1.y, k1.z, k1.w}; // For each local query #pragma unroll for (int iq = 0; iq < 8; ++iq) { float* q_row = q_smem[q_local_start + iq]; // Compute partial dot product float s = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < 8; ++d) { s = fmaf(q_row[lane * 8 + d], k_reg[d], s); } // Warp reduce #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { s += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, s, offset); } // Lane 0 updates online softmax and computes weights if (lane == 0) { float m_prev = m_arr[iq]; m_arr[iq] = fmaxf(m_prev, s); float scale_prev = expf(m_prev - m_arr[iq]); float exp_s = expf(s - m_arr[iq]); float l_prev = l_arr[iq]; l_arr[iq] = l_prev * scale_prev + exp_s; // We need to broadcast weight and o_scale to all threads smem_broadcast[0] = exp_s / l_arr[iq]; smem_broadcast[1] = (l_prev * scale_prev) / l_arr[iq]; } __syncwarp(); float weight = smem_broadcast[0]; float o_scale = smem_broadcast[1]; // Load V[j] for this thread's 8 channels const float* v_ptr = V + kv_base + j * D + lane * 8; float4 v0 = ((const float4*)v_ptr)[0]; float4 v1 = ((const float4*)(v_ptr + 4))[0]; // Update output accumulators o_arr[iq][0] = o_arr[iq][0] * o_scale + weight * v0.x; o_arr[iq][1] = o_arr[iq][1] * o_scale + weight * v0.y; o_arr[iq][2] = o_arr[iq][2] * o_scale + weight * v0.z; o_arr[iq][3] = o_arr[iq][3] * o_scale + weight * v0.w; o_arr[iq][4] = o_arr[iq][4] * o_scale + weight * v1.x; o_arr[iq][5] = o_arr[iq][5] * o_scale + weight * v1.y; o_arr[iq][6] = o_arr[iq][6] * o_scale + weight * v1.z; o_arr[iq][7] = o_arr[iq][7] * o_scale + weight * v1.w; } } // Write output #pragma unroll for (int iq = 0; iq < 8; ++iq) { int global_q = q_start + q_local_start + iq; if (global_q < N) { float* o_ptr = O + qo_base + global_q * D + lane * 8; float4 o0, o1; o0.x = o_arr[iq][0]; o0.y = o_arr[iq][1]; o0.z = o_arr[iq][2]; o0.w = o_arr[iq][3]; o1.x = o_arr[iq][4]; o1.y = o_arr[iq][5]; o1.z = o_arr[iq][6]; o1.w = o_arr[iq][7]; ((float4*)o_ptr)[0] = o0; ((float4*)(o_ptr + 4))[0] = o1; } } } ``` Register usage: - m_arr[8], l_arr[8], o_arr[8][8] = 8 + 8 + 64 = 80 floats. - k_reg[8] = 8 floats - q_row pointer, s, various temporaries. - Total: ~100 registers. That's fine! Under 255 limit. Wait, o_arr[8][8] is 64 floats. Each float is one register. That's a lot but acceptable. Plus the other arrays. Actually, the compiler might not store all this in registers if unrolling doesn't work perfectly. But with `#pragma unroll`, it should try to keep them in registers. The iq loop inside the j loop: `for (int iq = 0; iq < 8; ++iq)`. This is 8 iterations. For each iteration, we have loads from Q_smem (pointer chases), FMAs, warp shuffles, etc. The compiler should unroll this if we add `pragma unroll`. But wait - unrolling 8 iterations with the inner body being quite large (including syncwarp, V loads, 8 FMAs per output channel) might be fine. The j loop runs 512 times and cannot be unrolled (too large). The iq loop inside j should be unrolled though. One concern: `__syncwarp()` inside the unrolled iq loop. If iq is unrolled, we'd have 8 `__syncwarp()` instructions inside a single j iteration. Is that okay? Yes, each warp processes its 8 queries sequentially, and syncwarp ensures all threads in the warp are at the same point. Actually, all threads in the warp execute the same iq loop iterations together (since iq is the same for all threads), so there's no divergence. Wait, but `iq` is loop counter, same for all threads. Yes, all 32 threads in the warp are executing the same iq iteration. The `__shfl_down_sync` and `__syncwarp()` are warp-synchronous operations, and since all threads participate, it's fine. The `__syncwarp()` after the warp reduction is needed because lane 0 updates some shared variables that other lanes will read. But actually, in warp-synchronous programming, the compiler usually handles this, and since we're in the same divergent-free branch, it might not even be needed. But to be safe, we keep it. Wait, but if we store in `smem_broadcast` (shared memory), we need to ensure lane 0's write is visible to other lanes in the same warp. With a `__syncwarp()`, all preceding memory operations from all threads in the warp are completed before any thread proceeds. So yes, it's needed (or the compiler barrier `__threadfence_block()` could work, but syncwarp is more appropriate here). Actually, for shared memory within a warp, `__syncwarp()` is overkill but safe. Since there are no cross-warp dependencies at all, we don't need `__syncthreads()`. **Potential issue:** The `smem_broadcast` array is shared memory. If multiple warps use the same indices, they'd overwrite each other. So we need either: - A warp-private shared memory region: `smem_broadcast[warp_id][2]` - Or use `__shfl_sync` to broadcast without shared memory! Using `__shfl_sync` is better - no shared memory needed for broadcast. ```cuda float weight, o_scale; if (lane == 0) { // compute weight and o_scale float w = ...; float s = ...; weight = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, w, 0); o_scale = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, s, 0); } else { weight = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, 0.0f, 0); // doesn't matter, broadcasting from lane 0 o_scale = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, 0.0f, 0); } ``` Actually, `__shfl_sync` can be written more simply as: ```cuda float weight, o_scale; // computed in lane 0 weight = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, weight, 0); o_scale = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, o_scale, 0); ``` All threads call __shfl_sync with `srcLane=0`, which broadcasts lane 0's value to all lanes. For lane 0, it returns its own value. This completely eliminates the need for shared memory for broadcast! We still need q_smem (128 KB), and that's it for shared memory. Great! Actually, I realize I have to be very careful about grid dimensions. Grid: (N / TILE_N, B * H) = (16, 1024) Block: 128 threads = 4 warps. Let me write the complete kernel: ```cuda __global__ void attention_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ Q, const float* __restrict__ K, const float* __restrict__ V, float* __restrict__ O, n int B, int H, int N, int D) { int bh = blockIdx.y; int b = bh / H; int h = bh % H; int tile_q = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid & 31; int warp_id = tid >> 5; int q_start = tile_q * 32; // TILE_N = 32 int qo_offset = ((b * H + h) * N + q_start) * D; int kv_offset = ((b * H + h) * N) * D; const float* q_ptr = Q + qo_offset; const float* k_base = K + kv_offset; const float* v_base = V + kv_offset; float* o_ptr_base = O + qo_offset; __shared__ float q_smem[32][1024]; // Load Q tile - 32*1024 = 32768 floats, 128 threads -> 256 floats each = 64 float4 // Each thread loads: tid, tid+128, tid+256, tid+384, ... // Wait, total float4 = 32 * 256 = 8192. Each thread loads 8192/128 = 64 float4. #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 64; ++i) { int idx = tid + i * 128; int row = idx >> 8; // idx / 256 int col4 = idx & 255; // idx % 256 if (row < 32 && q_start + row < N) { ((float4*)q_smem[row])[col4] = ((const float4*)(q_ptr + row * D))[col4]; } } __syncthreads(); int queries_per_warp = 32 >> 2; // 8 int q_local = warp_id * queries_per_warp; float m_arr[8]; n float l_arr[8]; float o_acc[8][8]; #pragma unroll for (int iq = 0; iq < 8; ++iq) { m_arr[iq] = -INFINITY; l_arr[iq] = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < 8; ++d) { o_acc[iq][d] = 0.0f; } } float scale = rsqrtf((float)D); // Main loop over keys for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { // Load K[j] for this thread's 8 channels const float* k_row = k_base + j * D + lane * 8; float4 k0 = ((const float4*)k_row)[0]; float4 k1 = ((const float4*)(k_row + 4))[0]; float k_reg[8] = {k0.x, k0.y, k0.z, k0.w, k1.x, k1.y, k1.z, k1.w}; n #pragma unroll for (int iq = 0; iq < 8; ++iq) { const float* q_row = q_smem[q_local + iq]; float s = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < 8; ++d) { s = fmaf(q_row[lane * 8 + d], k_reg[d], s); } // Warp reduce #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { s += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, s, offset); } float weight, o_scale; if (lane == 0) { float m_prev = m_arr[iq]; m_arr[iq] = fmaxf(m_prev, s * scale); float scale_prev = expf(m_prev - m_arr[iq]); float exp_s = expf(s * scale - m_arr[iq]); float l_prev = l_arr[iq]; l_arr[iq] = l_prev * scale_prev + exp_s; weight = exp_s / l_arr[iq]; o_scale = (l_prev * scale_prev) / l_arr[iq]; } weight = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, weight, 0); o_scale = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, o_scale, 0); // Load V[j] and accumulate const float* v_row = v_base + j * D + lane * 8; float4 v0 = ((const float4*)v_row)[0]; float4 v1 = ((const float4*)(v_row + 4))[0]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < 8; ++d) { o_acc[iq][d] = o_acc[iq][d] * o_scale; } o_acc[iq][0] += weight * v0.x; o_acc[iq][1] += weight * v0.y; o_acc[iq][2] += weight * v0.z; o_acc[iq][3] += weight * v0.w; o_acc[iq][4] += weight * v1.x; o_acc[iq][5] += weight * v1.y; o_acc[iq][6] += weight * v1.z; o_acc[iq][7] += weight * v1.w; } } // Write output #pragma unroll for (int iq = 0; iq < 8; ++iq) { int global_q = q_start + q_local + iq; n if (global_q < N) { float* o_row = o_ptr_base + iq * D + lane * 8; float4 o0, o1; o0.x = o_acc[iq][0]; o0.y = o_acc[iq][1]; o0.z = o_acc[iq][2]; o0.w = o_acc[iq][3]; o1.x = o_acc[iq][4]; o1.y = o_acc[iq][5]; o1.z = o_acc[iq][6]; o1.w = o_acc[iq][7]; ((float4*)o_row)[0] = o0; ((float4*)(o_row + 4))[0] = o1; } } } ``` Wait, there's a subtle issue. In the score computation, `s * scale` might overflow if s is large. But that's a standard concern. `scale = 1/sqrt(D)` where D=1024, so scale ~0.031. Multiplying by scale is fine. Also note: in the online softmax update, when `j == 0` and `m_prev == -INFINITY`: - `m_arr[iq] = fmaxf(-INFINITY, s * scale) = s * scale` - `scale_prev = expf(-INFINITY - m_new) = 0` - `exp_s = expf(s*scale - m_new) = expf(0) = 1` - `l_arr[iq] = 0 * 0 + 1 = 1` - `weight = 1 / 1 = 1` - `o_scale = (0 * 0) / 1 = 0` - So `o_acc = o_acc * 0 + 1 * V[j]` => `o_acc = V[j]` This works! All threads in a warp read `q_row[lane * 8 + d]` simultaneously for a fixed `d`. - Thread 0 reads q_row[0], q_row[1], ..., q_row[7] (banks 0, 1, ..., 7) - Thread 1 reads q_row[8], q_row[9], ..., q_row[15] (banks 8, 9, ..., 15) - ... - Thread 31 reads q_row[248], ..., q_row[255] (banks 24, ..., 31) For each `d`, 32 threads read 32 different banks. So threads 0, 4, 8, 12, 16, 20, 24, 28 all access bank d simultaneously! This is an 8-way bank conflict! Oh no! That's terrible for shared memory performance. To avoid bank conflicts, we need threads to access different banks. For float arrays, bank = (address / 4) % 32. We want bank[thread] = thread. That means address[thread] should have (address/4) % 32 = thread. So address = thread * 4 + k * 128. (128 bytes = 32 floats = one row of 32 banks). But our original code had q_row[lane * 8 + d] where lanes access offsets 0..7, 8..15, etc. This causes conflicts because 8-way stride isn't coprime with 32. For the FMA loop, we need consecutive threads to access consecutive floats, OR we need to use a different memory layout in SMEM. Option 1: Change Q SMEM layout to swizzled/transposed. Instead of `q_smem[32][1024]`, use `q_smem[32][1024]` but access as `q_smem[iq][lane + d * 32]` for d=0..31 (1024/32=32). - Thread lane accesses offset `lane + d*32`. - Bank = (lane + d*32) / 4 = lane/4 + d*8. For d=0: bank = lane/4. Thread 0-3 access bank 0, threads 4-7 access bank 1, etc. 4-way conflict! Hmm. Option 2: Use float4 loads and access bank-aligned. If each thread loads a float4 (4 consecutive floats), bank accesses would be lane*4+d for d=0..3. Actually, let's think about float4 access. For `((float4*)q_row)[lane]`: - Thread lane accesses float4 at offset `lane * 4` floats = `lane * 16` bytes. - Bank = (lane * 16) / 4 % 32 = lane * 4 % 32. - Thread 0: bank 0,1,2,3 (for the 4 floats in the float4) - Thread 1: bank 4,5,6,7 - Thread 2: bank 8,9,10,11 - Thread 4: bank 16,17,18,19 - Thread 8: bank 0,1,2,3 (since 8*4=32=0 mod 32) - Thread 8 and Thread 0 both access banks 0,1,2,3 simultaneously! Conflict! For a float4 per thread, if banks are 4-byte wide and there are 32 banks, a float4 occupies 4 consecutive banks. Adjacent threads accessing adjacent float4s will banks 0-3, 4-7, ..., 28-31, 0-3, 4-7, etc. There are no two threads accessing the same bank at the same time because: - Thread 0: banks 0,1,2,3 - Thread 1: banks 4,5,6,7 - ... - Thread 7: banks 28,29,30,31 - Thread 8: banks 0,1,2,3 Wait, thread 0 and thread 8 both want banks 0,1,2,3 simultaneously! This is a 4-way bank conflict (8 threads per bank group). To avoid this, we can use a swizzled layout. For example: Store Q as `q_smem[32][256]` of float4, and when loading from global memory, rearrange. If occupancy is too low (6%), memory latency isn't hidden well. Even though K+V reads are 64 GB, the effective bandwidth utilization might be poor, making the kernel slower. If occupancy is 43%, memory latency is better hidden, but K+V reads are 256 GB. Which is better? It depends on whether the kernel is memory-bound or compute-bound. For this attention kernel: - FLOPs per block = 32 queries * 512 keys * 2*1024 = 33,554,432 FMAs = 33.5 MFMAs - Memory per block: K+V = 4 MB (ignoring Q,O) - Arithmetic intensity = 33.5 MFMAs / 4 MB = 8.4 FLOPs/byte (if we count each FMA as 2 FLOPs: 16.8 FLOPs/byte) - H100 DRAM bandwidth: 3.35 TB/s - To saturate memory bandwidth, we need compute at 3.35 TB/s * 16.8 = 56.3 TFLOPS. - H100 FP32 compute: 67 TFLOPS. - So the kernel is roughly balanced between compute and memory! Let me implement it this way. Each thread: 1. Computes its own partial dot 2. Participates in warp reduction to get score (all will get score via shfl or warp reduce algorithm that returns to all threads) 3. Each thread computes m_new, l_new, update its own accumulators The warp reduction returning to all threads can be done with: ``` #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) partial += __shfl_xor_sync(mask, partial, offset); ``` After this, `partial` contains the full sum in ALL threads (not just lane 0), because shfl_xor is symmetric. Then each thread computes identical score, m_new, l_new, and updates its accumulators. No need for any shared memory at all for the softmax state! This is great.
