Let me design the kernel: ``` __global__ void attention_forward_kernel(const float* Q, const float* K, const float* V, float* O, int B, int H, int N, int D) { int bh = blockIdx.y; int b = bh / H; int h = bh % H; int q_tile = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int lane = tid & 31; int warp_id = tid >> 5; // 0-7 int num_queries = 8; // per block int q = q_tile * num_queries + warp_id; if (q >= N) return; int d_per_thread = 32; // 1024 / 32 threads = 32 channels per thread int d_start = lane * d_per_thread; // 0, 32, 64, ..., 992 // Base offsets int qo_offset = ((b * H + h) * N + q) * D + d_start; int kv_offset_base = ((b * H + h) * N) * D + d_start; const float* q_ptr = Q + qo_offset; const float* k_base = K + kv_offset_base; const float* v_base = V + kv_offset_base; float* o_ptr = O + qo_offset; // Load Q into registers (8 float4 = 32 floats) float4 q_regs[8]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; ++i) { q_regs[i] = ((const float4*)(q_ptr + i * 4))[0]; } // Convert to flat array for easier access float q_arr[32]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; ++i) { q_arr[i*4+0] = q_regs[i].x; q_arr[i*4+1] = q_regs[i].y; q_arr[i*4+2] = q_regs[i].z; q_arr[i*4+3] = q_regs[i].w; } // Output accumulators float o_acc[32]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 32; ++i) o_acc[i] = 0.0f; float m = -INFINITY; float l = 0.0f; float scale = rsqrtf((float)D); // Loop over all keys for (int j = 0; j < N; ++j) { // Load K[j] channels float4 k_regs[8]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; ++i) { k_regs[i] = ((const float4*)(k_base + j * D + i * 4))[0]; } // Compute partial dot product float partial = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; ++i) { partial = fmaf(q_regs[i].x, k_regs[i].x, partial); partial = fmaf(q_regs[i].y, k_regs[i].y, partial); partial = fmaf(q_regs[i].z, k_regs[i].z, partial); partial = fmaf(q_regs[i].w, k_regs[i].w, partial); } // Warp reduce to full score #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { partial += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, partial, offset); } float s = partial * scale; // Online softmax update (all threads compute identical values) float m_new = fmaxf(m, s); float exp_old = expf(m - m_new); float exp_s = expf(s - m_new); float l_new = l * exp_old + exp_s; float scale_o = (l * exp_old) / l_new; // l is l_old float w = exp_s / l_new; // Load V[j] channels float4 v_regs[8]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; ++i) { v_regs[i] = ((const float4*)(v_base + j * D + i * 4))[0]; } // Update accumulators #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; ++i) { o_acc[i*4+0] = o_acc[i*4+0] * scale_o + v_regs[i].x * w; o_acc[i*4+1] = o_acc[i*4+1] * scale_o + v_regs[i].y * w; o_acc[i*4+2] = o_acc[i*4+2] * scale_o + v_regs[i].z * w; o_acc[i*4+3] = o_acc[i*4+3] * scale_o + v_regs[i].w * w; } m = m_new; l = l_new; } // Normalize and write output float inv_l = 1.0f / l; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 8; ++i) { float4 o4; o4.x = o_acc[i*4+0] * inv_l; o4.y = o_acc[i*4+1] * inv_l; o4.z = o_acc[i*4+2] * inv_l; o4.w = o_acc[i*4+3] * inv_l; ((float4*)(o_ptr + i * 4))[0] = o4; } } ``` Wait, this is beautiful. It uses zero shared memory. Each warp handles one query. Each thread handles 32 channels. Each block has 8 warps = 256 threads, handling 8 queries. Let me verify the math: - 256 threads per block - 8 warps per block - 1 query per warp - 8 queries per block - Number of blocks = N/8 * B*H = 64 * 1024 = 65536 Per query: - 32 threads * 32 channels = 1024 channels ✓ Memory reads per block per iteration (one key): - K: 256 threads * 32 channels * 4 bytes = 32KB - V: same = 32KB - Total per iteration: 64KB - 512 iterations: 32MB per block Wait, each block handles 8 queries and loads K and V once per key, so per key it loads 1024*4*2 = 8KB, but each thread loads 32 channels = 128 bytes, times 256 threads = 32KB of K and 32KB of V = 64KB per key. Times 512 = 32MB per block. Total memory reads across all blocks: - 65536 blocks * 32MB = 2,097,152 MB = 2,048 TB This is the same as the previous kernel. Each query reads all K and V. Total Q reads: 32*32*512*1024*4 = 2GB. Total K+V reads: 32*32*512*512*1024*4*2 = 4TB. Actually that's for all queries reading all KV. But PyTorch naive would also have similar memory traffic (actually much worse because it writes intermediate P matrix). Wait, PyTorch `scaled_dot_product_attention` on H100 will likely use FlashAttention-style kernel which also reads K and V once per query. So our memory traffic is the same as FlashAttention. The key advantage of our kernel over a naive three-pass approach is fused memory access. Compared to PyTorch's optimized `scaled_dot_product_attention`, we need to see if our implementation is competitive. One concern: with 32MB per block and 65536 blocks, total is indeed ~2PB of reads. But since each SM processes many blocks and K/V are in memory, the actual HBM bandwidth is what matters. For the whole attention: - Q read once: 2GB - K read 512 times (once per query): 2GB * 512 = 1024GB - V read 512 times: 1024GB - O write once: 2GB Total: ~2050GB = 2TB At 3.35 TB/s, this is ~0.6ms for memory-bound. But with compute of ~1B FLOPs, at 67 TFLOPS this is ~0.015ms. So it's definitely memory bound. FlashAttention's key optimization is tiling to keep data in shared/ SRAM and reuse it across multiple queries. With our approach, reading K and V once per key for a tile of queries is already good, but we read them 512 times (once per query across all queries). FlashAttention processes K and V in tiles, and multiple queries share those tiles. Our block processes 8 queries together and reads all K and V once. But then another block for the next 8 queries reads all K and V again. FlashAttention would process more queries per block and reuse K/V tiles across them, but that also doesn't eliminate the total reads. Actually, FlashAttention's main benefit is NOT reducing HBM reads (the total HBM reads are Q+K+V+O for both), but rather reducing HBM WRITES (no intermediate P matrix). In our kernel, we also don't write intermediate P. So I think our kernel should be competitive with or better than PyTorch's implementation, especially if PyTorch falls back to a non-FlashAttention implementation for this shape. Let me compile and test this new kernel. However, the current working kernel already has online softmax fused! Let me look at it again... Actually no, the working kernel computes scores for all 512 keys, stores in shared memory, does softmax, then loads V values. This also doesn't write to global memory. It's just that the online softmax version eliminates storing scores in shared memory and does the weighted accumulation as it goes. The online version should be better because: 1. No shared memory needed for scores (saves ~2KB) 2. Better arithmetic intensity because we compute and consume scores immediately 3. Potentially better ILP Let me compile the new online softmax kernel. One more optimization: Instead of using 8 float4 loads per thread (32 channels), maybe use 4 float8 or just 16 float2? float4 is good for coalescing. Let me also think about register pressure: 8 * q + 8 * k + 8 * v + 32 * o_acc + m + l + ... that's about 64 floats = 64 registers. Plus loop counters. Should be well under 255. Actually wait, each float4 is 4 floats in a register, but the compiler might handle it differently. 8 float4 variables might use 8 vector registers. Let me code this up and compile.
